02 November 2012 (1st round): This CAR relates to the term “accuracy” in the flow instruments Technical Specification (VCSMR1.32A) and “Maximum Permissible Error” as used in the calibration reports issued by Endress+Hauser for instruments tagged as FIR/TIR/PIR (VCSMR1.33.G/I/L, VCSMR1.34 G/K/N, and VCMR1.35 D/H/K)  In order to respond to this CAR, we requested clarification from Endress+Hauser and were explained the following: (Please note that signed statement to this effect is still being sought from Endress+Hauser) 
(a)
 
The term “accuracy” used in the technical specification relates to the instrument measurement accuracy when tested using laboratory / test gas under laboratory conditions. Whereas these terms are useful to compare the quality of one instrument to another (during purchasing decision for example), the accuracy can not be replicated under “operating conditions” for non-laboratory gas. As such, the accuracy expectation given in the technical specification can not be used as an 
2012-11-14 (1st round):  a)
 
OK. This can be accepted. Thus, for the term “accuracy” the same unit is tested several times, measured values are compared with design values. Finally, a statement can be given how accurate the unit resp. the device works under test (i.e. labaratory) conditions.         
b)
 
OK. As Project Participant did not request any higher tolerance of reading, manufacturer’s default value of 0.5% for the Maximum Permissible Error is used. This can be accepted.      
c)
 
This statement can be accepted. All devices used for flow determination (i.e. flow meter, pressure sensor, thermometer) have been inspected in December 2011 (see as evidences VCSMR1.33.G/I/L, VCSMR1.34 G/K/N, and VCMR1.35 D/H/K). All meters passed the test which means all devices were found to be below manufacturer’s default value of 0.5% for the Maximum Permissible Error. 
Thus,  calibration  was  neither  necessary  nor  conducted,  but the revised VCS Monitoring Report (version 2.0) often

operational-performance benchmark of the instrument itself. 
  
(b)
 
The manufacturer also clarify that the term “Maximum Permissible Error or MPE” refers to the tolerance of reading that is deemed “acceptable” by the end-user, NOT by the manufacturer.  
 For instrument with commercial trading implications, MPE is generally agreed and stipulated in the commercial contracts. Where the end-user does not provide this information, Endress+Hauser sets an MPE value as per generally accepted measurement tolerance limit, in our case +/-0.5%.   
(c)
 
During calibration, Endress+Hauser will only re-calibrate the instrument when the reading were found to be above the MPE. Where inaccuracy were found to be below the MPE, calibration is deemed not necessary, and only the reading is reported. This protocol is in compliance with the requirement of ISO/IEC17025 as stated in the “Calibration Report” issued by Endress+Hauser.

30 November 2012 (2nd round):  Clarification of the value of the applied benchmark
 
We  shall  clarify  that  the  Power  Plant  (in  it’s  entirety,  not  viewed  as  individual  engine)  is  considered  “not-operating”  when  at  any  given  
minute
  it  does  not  
withdraw  any  gas  from  the  main  supply.  Thus  the  determination  of  downtime  being  
1Nm3  is  reviewed  
on  minute  basis
.  Thus,  the  applied  benchmark  is  
1Nm3/min, not
 1Nm3/hour  
 The  value  in  the  “raw  data”  however  presents  flow-rate at any given minute as Nm3/hr. Thus, in order to achieve  the  equivalent  Nm3/min,  this  value  needs  to  be divided by the 60minutes of an hour.  
For  example,  in  the  period  stated  by  the  auditor,  the  flow-rate  to  the  power  house  is  indicated  to  be  2Nm3/hour  in  Sheet(“Raw  Data”). Thus, in that particular minute, gas is flowing  at  a  rate  of  0.03Nm3/min  –  as  such  the Power Plant is considered not operating.   
We  would  like  to  re-confirm  that  the  applied  cut-off  
2012-12-06 (2nd round):  OK.  The  clarification  as  provided  by  Project  Participant  can  be  deemed  sufficient.  When  considering  that  the  recorded value of LFG flow (i.e. 2 Nm³/h) must be divided by   60   (as   the   value   is   displayed   in   Nm³/h   but   the   recording  is  doing  minutewise),  the  resulting  flow  cannot  be  used  to  operate  any  gas  engine.  Thus,  the  whole  power plant can be considered to be out of operation.  Additionally,  the  approach  to  consider  a  value  of  
≤
1 
Nm
3
/min  as  benchmark  can  be  followed.  As  soon  as  the  
values  are  going  to  be  higher  than  
≤
1  Nm³/min  (or  its  
equivalent  of  
≤
60  Nm³/h),  it  is  evident  that  the  solenoid  
valve in the pipeline feeding the power plant has given the order  to  open  (for  start,  gas  is  then  sucked  to  start  any  engine)  or  the  valve  is  not  yet  closed  (as  any  engine  is  about to stop). Thus in that cases the plant is considered to be in operation.

Whether  or  not  we  set  the  benchmark  at  0.01Nm3  per minute or 1.5Nm3 per minute is however a highly subjective discussion.  The intention to put a number is  simply  to  provide  clarity  of  the  boundary  of  the  mathematical   problem,   which   is   a   more   robust   practice  than  setting  a  numerical  0  Nm3  per  minute. CAR 31 (2012-10-19) During    on-site    verification,    several    inconsistencies (e.g. for 3 months) were found  between  the  converted  raw  data  and   the   raw   data   presented   in   the   monthly ER files.  
02 November 2012 (1st round): The error was due to the limitation of the number of data points that can be handled by the processing software. To reach a monthly aggregation, data must be collated several times, and this is prone to human error. As a response to this, in the subsequent design of data storage and reporting, data is downloaded and collated using excel automation on daily basis.    The error has now been corrected. Thank you. 
2012-11-14 (1st round): OK.  While  on  site,  Project  Participant  downloaded  all  available  raw  data  from  project’s  data  log  hardware  in  presence  of  GLC's  Assessment  Team.  Data  has  been  handed over to the GLC's Assessment Team. In a second step,   data   was   processed   by   Auditors   by   using   the   specialized  software  to  reproduce  the  presented  monthly  data.  As  indicated  by  the  finding,  some  inconsistencies  have  been  found,  namely  for  the  months  of  June  2011,  August  2011  and  Feb  2012. CAR 32 (2012-10-19) The Calibration of the raw gas analyzer used  to  monitor  the  parameter  wCH4  was   delayed.   Conservative   approach   should   be   applied   for   measurement   results according to UNFCCC guideline.

2012-11-14 (1st round): The   first   available   calibration   report   during   project   operation    dates    2012-04-12    (VCSMR1.29    GA    01.    2012APR12  QIAL  weekly  calibration.pdf).  The  methane  analyzer is showing a concentration of 55.9 % of methane while  using  a  test  gas  with  a  certified  gas  quality  of  60.1 %. It  is  not  clear  how  this  fact  has  been  considered   for   the   period   without   any   documented   calibration  for  the  methane  analyzer  (from  2011-02-26  to  2012-04-12). In   the   first   calibration   report,   the   gas   analyzer   indicates a methane concentration of 55.9% for a gas with a known concentration of 60.1% (in the standard gas).   This  implies  that  the  gas  analyzers  under-report  the  amount  of  methane  in  landfill  gas  by  4.2%,  which  is  beyond  the  maximum  permissible  error  of  +/-2%.  As  consequence    to    this,    the    calculated    baseline    emission  from  methane  captured  from  landfill  gas  is  already most conservative.    
2012-12-06 (2nd round): OK.  When  comparing  the  value  measured  (55.9  %)  while  a  certified   standard   gas   was   supplied   to   the   methane   analyzer  with  a  concentration  of  60.1  %,  it  can  be  seen  that the actual indication is wi
th -4.2 % lower (in absolute 
numbers)  and  even  with  -7.0  %  
lower,  if  calculated  to  a  
relative   number.   This   means   that   the   analyzer   was   constantly reporting a lower va
lue than the real value was. 
Thus,  this  error  is  greater  than  the  maximum  permissible  error  of  ±  2.0  %.  As  this  error  is  showing  lower  values,  this  error  does  not  lead  to  higher  emission  reductions.  Thus,  the  application  of  the  measured  values  is  already  conservative, as a deduction of 7 % is already apllied due to the occurred error.  Hence, CAR 32 is closed.

parameter,    and    if    so,    how    it    is    monitored   and   how   the   monitoring   result  is  considered  in  flare  efficiency  determination.  Clarification  should  also  be     given     regarding     how     flare’s     operational  range  of  gas  flow  rate  is  considered       in       flare       efficiency       determination.  
that the correct range of flare operational temperature should be between 800-1200degC, instead of the earlier thought 1,000 to 1,200degC. This has now been corrected in all spreadsheet and the VCSMR1.   The combustion temperature is measured by S-type thermocouple, and auto-monitored and controlled by a Programmable Logic Controller (PLC) to always operate to reach an optimum (dynamic) set point between 1,000 to 1,200 degC, by varying air intake as soon as sufficient pres
sure (flow) is detected.  
This PLC’s set point are controlled & password protected by the manufacturer and can not be changed by the project operator. Thus, monitoring only constitutes whether or not the PLC is functioning as intended.  If the PLC is well functioning then the exhaust gas temperature (Tflare) will reach the higher end of the limit without exceeding 1,200degC, and simple observation of the parameter Tflare reading can deduce the state of the PLC.  The PDD and the methodology determined the flare efficiency from exhaust gas temperature, not by the combustion temperature.  
document that “flare combustion temperature is a part of the ‘flare operational temperature’”. 
-
 
Please provide an evidence to the statement of the manufacturer “that the correct range of flare operational temperature should be between 800-1200degC”. This statement is in contradiction with the design of the flare which indicates a combustion temperature of 1,000 up to 1,200 °C (VCSMR1.39B, page 19). 
-
 
While VCSMR1.35B is showing a maximal designed flow of 2,625 Nm³/h, it is not clear for what flowmeter (serial number) this calculation was provided. Additionally, the maximal designed flow of 2,625 Nm³/h is contradictory to the designed flow of the flare booster station as per VCSMR1.39B, page 19. 
 Hence, CAR 33 is still open.

 The    maximum    flow    of    the    flow-meter    depends  on  the  pipe  (standard)  diameter  of  which the DC unit is fitted. As consequence there  is  slight  lapse  between  the  flow-meter  capacity  (2,625Nm3/hr)  and  the  capacity  of  the  flare  unit  (2,500Nm3/hr).  As  with  any  system,  the  smaller  capacity  becomes  the  limiting    capacity,    and    hence    the    real    capacity of the entire system.  
consistent  with  the  design  of  the  flare  which  indicates  a  combustion  temperature  of  1,000  up  to 1,200 °C (VCSMR1.39B, page 19). 
c)
 
While  comparing  the  documents  VCSMR1.35B  and  VCSMR1.35L  it  becomes  evident,  that  the  indicated  flow  of  2,625  Nm³/h  is  the  maximum  flow capacity which the combination of inner pipe diameter  and  installed  flow  measuring  devices  are  designed  for.  The  normal  range  of  this  flow  measuring   device   is   indicated   as   between   500...2,500   Nm³/h.   It   is   understood   that   the   maximum  capacity  must  be  slightly  higher  to  compensate operational events (such as sudden release   of   condensate   inside   the   LFG-piping   system  may  increase  the  flowrate  for  a  short  time).  Thus,  the  limit  of  2,500  Nm³/h  can  be  accepted.   
Hence, CAR 33 is closed.

CAR 35 (2012-10-19) The  names  of  the  flow  rate  parameters  slightly deviate from the names in PDD. 
02 November 2012 (1st round): The name changes reflects the time interval of how the data is recorded. The PDD stated that volume is to be reported every hour, whereas the Project uses a much higher data recordin
g resolution (per minute). 
These differentiation improves the accuracy of reporting rather than the other way around, as such does not constitute a deviation.  The name changes are now explained in the “any comment” section of the respective flow parameter.  
2012-11-14 (1st round): OK.  Project  Participant’s  answer  can  be  accepted.  As  explained by Project Participant, the higher data recording frequency leads to higher data accuracy and thus is more conservative    in    terms    of    calculation    of    emission    reductions.  Hence, CAR 35 is closed.

any   information   on   events   that   may   impact the GHG emission reductions or removals and monitoring. “ While  the  presented  table  is  marked  as  “major       milestones       relevant       for       implementation”,  it  is  not  clear  if  this  table  should  be  seen  as  information  regarding  the  operation.  Additionally,  it  is  not  clear  why  milestones  are  not  completely set in chronological order.  
02 November 2012 (1st round): Each of the milestones presented in the table are affecting the implementation status and thus the operation of the project, and subsequently it’s ability to claim emission reduction.  For example,  
(a)
 
the acceptance of each new gas engines & its certification by t
he authority (whichever 
date the later) determines increased operation capacity of the Project, and thus it’s ability to displaced grid electricity.  
(b)
 
the completeness of commissioning of the degassing station means that the project commences the counting on it’s methane abatement ability, thus supporting the information outlined in Section 4 (Table 1) where methane abatement commenced only in June 2012.  
 Accordingly, the table has now been re-titled to: “Major milestones relevant to the implementation and thus operation of the emission reduction project”, to emphasize this effect.  Each milestone has now been rearranged in chronological order to the date of the document.  
2012-11-14 (1st round): GLC's   Assessment   Team   reviewed   the   revised   VCS   Monitoring Report (version 2.0) with a special focus on its section 2.1 (Implementation Status of the Project Activity). While the implementation status of the Project Activity has been   clearly   and   sufficiently   described,   operational   information  is  still  not  suffici
ently  presented  in  regard  of  
“any  information  on  events  that  may  impact  the  GHG  emission reductions or removals and monitoring”.   It  can  be  confirmed  that  the  table  in  section  2.1  is  now  presenting      “Major      milestones      relevant      to      the      implementation   and   thus   op
eration   of   the   emission   
reduction  project”  and  that  the  milestones  have  been  rearranged to chronological order.  Hence, CL 4 is still open.

CL 11 (2012-10-19) * In  some  months,  raw  data  are  not  fully  available,  i.e.  less  than  44,640  resp.  43,200  values  for  a  31  resp.  30  days  period.  It  is  not  clear  why  data  are  missing.   
02 November 2012 (1st round): When the power plant lost it’s grid connectivity, it losses it’s entire power supply, including those used for the data-logger. As such, the number of data sets are lower than the maximum number possible for every given months.  This lower number of data sets, however, has no impact to the emission reduction claimed as absence of data means that emission reduction can-not be claimed in the first place.   
2012-11-14 (1st round): OK.  As  described  in  CAR  31,  GLC's  Assessment  Team  checked the available raw data. So, DOE can confirm that some  monthly  data  sets  do  not  provide  the  maximum  possible  quantity  of  data  being  registered  every  minute.  The   missing   time   is   justified   by   the   lost   of   power   connection. These events can be traced in the events log of the specialized software as “Power on” and “Power off”. 
 
As    Project    Participant    emphasizes,    no    emission    reductions  are  claimed  for  times  logged  data  cannot  be  presented.  
Ok/Closed

2012-11-14 (1st round): OK. The revised VCS Monitoring Report (version 2.0) now states  a  crediting  period  from  2011-02-26  to  2033-07-31.  The   VCS   standard   in   its   version   3.2   provides   the   guidelines for determination of the project crediting period. Section   3.9.3   of   the   VCS   Standard   (v3.2)   gives   an   example  of  the  eligibility  for  projects  under  VCS  and  one  other Greenhouse-Gas-Programs, such as CDM. Thus, a CDM  project  with  a  seven  year  twice  renewable  project  crediting  period  is  not  eligible  for  VCU  issuance  beyond  the  end  of  those  21  years.  This  means  for  the  Project  Activity, that the end of the 21 years is marks as well the end  of  the  VCS  crediting  period,
